Appendice VI
CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
INTERNATIONAL STUDY INSTITUTE ON VICTIMOLOGY 
BELLAGIO, ITALY, JULY 1-12, 1975 
(Conclusioni e raccomandazioni 
del Convegno Intemazionale di studi sulla vittimologia 
Bellagio, Italia, 1-12 luglio 1975) (*)
(*) Reprinted from Victimology : an International Journal, vol. I, N. 1, 
Spring 1976, pp. 130-153 © Copyright 1976 Visage Press, Inc., 3049 Wisconsin 
Avenue N. W., Washington D.C. 20016 USA. All rights reserved.
I. Theoretical Perspectives
This section was prepared by a task force composed of Vahakn 
Dadrian (USA), Israel Drapkin (Israel), Patricia Francis (USA), Debo­
rah Galvin (USA), Ellen Hill (Italy), Stanley W. Johnston (Australia), 
Erik P. Kibuka (Uganda), Beniamin Mendelsohn (Israel), Charles Ohaeri 
(Nigeria), David Smith (USA), and Diane Vaughan (USA).
The victimological theory group approached its task from two major 
perspectives: theory and policy.
The group discussed different theoretical perspectives of the science 
of victimology. There was a general feeling that because of the variety 
of disciplines involved, and because of the developmental stage of 
victimology, it would be unwise to propose any one definition as compre­
hensive. There was also a deside to avoid the pitfail of excluding 
potentially valuable aspects or contributions to the field. Thus, instead 
of attempting to build a total theory, the group directed its efforts to 
sketching major theoretical perspectives. The narrowest definition of 
the scope of victimology attempts to understand it as a subdiscipline 
of criminology focusing on victims of crime. The broadest definition 
would reach out to embrace all forms of suffering or harm, irrespective 
of origin. It is hoped that patterns of research and theorizing will in 
the future bridge these two positions.

---

1'64
LA VITTIMA
Theoretical framework
The theoretical perspective group approached the task by: first, 
reviewing opposed approaches to victimology; second, presenting con­
ceptual frameworks for optimal theoretical development; third attempting 
to define and establish parameters in substantive areas of victimology; 
fourth, analyzing some of the processes of victimization. The working 
perspective was historical and longitudinal as well as cross-sectional 
and analytic.
Few researchers and practitioners bridge all of the areas of victimol­
ogy, but an awareness of the potential overlap can be helpful. Victimol­
ogy, like other more mature disciplines, has struggled to develop a 
general definition that does more than announce that it “is the study 
of ...”
A model of the social processes involved in the study of the 
victimization process must begin with a conceptualization of the universe 
of act and actions that form the basic subject matter. Once the para­
meters of this universe are at least broadly identified then more specific 
patterns can be isolated for explication. The general patterns of defi­
nition creation, definition application, post-victimization behavior, and 
systems for dealing with victims, and the linkage between these areas 
were discussed by the task force.
So far it can only be stated that the universe of events that have 
some probability of being defined as victimizing, conceptually define 
the parameters of victimology. Beyond this admittedly somewhat circular 
definition, little consensus has emerged. To include all pain, suffering or 
strain seems going too far. While self-identification, control agents or 
significant others provide clearer definitions or identifications, strong 
agreement is lacking. Like in all social processes, it is assumed that 
the social situation, the prior interactions and the immediate interaction 
dynamics are necessary elements in the explanation of social events.
Topics of interest
Topics of prevailing interest to the victimologist which have 
relevance and significance emphasize theoretical perspectives, empirical 
research, and policy formation. The list is niether exhaustive nor 
necessarily unbiased:
— community, societal and international reactions to cases of 
victimization
— 
contemporary sale of children into slavery
— 
government-induced victimization
— 
institutionalized victimization

---

APPENDICE VI
165
— the prison inmate as victim
— institutionalized juvenile delinquents and juvenile role offenders 
in victim aggressor relationships
— liberty of expression
— misuse of scientific experimentation with humans
— misuse of social engineering (behavioral and hard sciences)
— misuse of the medical profession (e.g., mental institutions for 
political purposes)
— neutralization processes of victims
— pursuit of power, achievement and success
— rates of victimization as indicators of social normality and 
pathology
— rassessment of existing theories of group victimization
— psychological and socio-psychological dimensions of victimizat­
ion (especially fear and distrust)
— sexual victimization
— individual/group dynamics of victimization
— the functional importance of victims
— the functions of victims in an achievement-oriented society
— victimity as an ormai societal phenomenon
— validation of theoretical constants.
Recommendations
The task force developed the following recommendations for 
intervention :
1) That countries submit unreservedly to the jurisdiction of the 
International Court of Justice, the United Nations Human Rights 
Committee, and the United Nations Committee on the Elimination of 
Racial Discrimination;
2) That the General Assembly of the United Nations renew work 
on the 1954 drafts for a World Criminal Code and a standing World 
Criminal Court with a view to the early prosecution of offenders for 
the crime of aggression;
3) That countries which have not ratified the Genocide Convention 
of 1948 do so; and
4) That governments avoid victimization of their own citizens 
such as political opponents, prison inmates and others by torture or 
maltreatment; and specifically, that governments avoid inflicting on 
their own citizens and arbitrary abrogation of their constitutional or 
human rights.

---

166
LA VITTIMA
II. Research and Methodology
This section summarizes the conclusions resulting from a meeting 
of a group of social science researchers from different countries who 
had undertaken systematic quantitative research on victims of crime. 
Their names are: Leonard Bickman (USA), Lynn Curtis (USA), Jan 
Fiselier (Netherlands), Hazel Genn (UK), Gerd F. Kirchoff (W. Germ­
any). Murray A. Straus (USA), Glora Count van Manen (USA), Irvin 
Waller (Canada), and Kristen Williams (USA). They have set out 
a list of the principal methods that have been used, outlined broadly 
their advantages and disadvantages, and examined in detail some 
of the methodological problems associated with victimization surveys 
and archival data. Research findings and their implications have been 
reviewed and new directions for the future suggested.
Table 1 reviews some of the typical methods used in victim 
research.

vation; anecdotal; deduc­
tive theoretical
Low
Low
Low
Low
Hypothesis generation; pheno­
menology of victim process

---

APPENDICE VI
167
There are five broad headings: Survey Methods; Archival Methods 
(e.g., police or coroner’s records); Observational Methods; Experimental 
Designs; and Nonquantitative Research. To be useful in developing 
theory, guiding prevention or treatment, and modifying the criminal 
justice system these methods must pass the criteria of confidence in 
causal relations and generalizability, as shown in Table 1. In addition, 
they will be constrained by cost and ethical considerations. As a guide 
to persons interested in research on victims, each of the four methods 
is rated as “high”, “medium,” or “low” in terms of how well they 
have been judged to pass the criteria, their cost, and the extent to 
which they raise ethical problems. The uses and advantages of each 
are summarized.
Probably the most important innovative research method associated 
with victims has been the victimization survey. Individuare, households 
and businesses have been surveyed to find out if they have been a 
victim or not. The surveys have been conducted in a number of different 
countries. They can, however, be used to measure extent of victimization 
only in instances where persons know that they have in fact been 
victimized. The primary problems of victimization surveys are not 
significantly different from those of any retrospective social survey. To 
some extent, the specific problems will depend on the aims of the survey.
The major aims of victim surveys are:
— a more accurate measurement of the incidence of criminal 
victimization, and
— the investigation of the impact of criminal activity on the popul­
ation at large (for example, fear of crime, concern about rising crime 
rate).
For a number of reasons victim surveys are considered to be superior 
to police statistics of recorded crime. Police statistics represent only 
the final stage in the process of producing crime rates. This process 
includes the perception of acts as crimes by victims; their definition 
of the acts as certain types of crime, the victim’s decision to report 
the crimes to the police; redefinition of the events by the police; and 
the decision by the police to record the events as crime. At every stage 
in this processi here is a nonrandom loss which eventually leads to a 
distorted picture of crime. Although much of this loss may be trivial, 
serious crimes such as sex offences and white collar crime are rarely 
reported and are thus excluded from the total picture of crime.
There are a number of reasons why victimization surveys are 
conducted. First, there is the social indicator function: If the moral 
health of societies is to be judged on the basis of crime rates, it is 
better to use estimates of the level of crime which are not distorted in 

---

168
LA VITTIMA
the abovementioned way. Second, there is the crime prevention and 
program evaluation function: The problem of crime prevention is 
approached by a shift in emphasis from offender characteristics to 
victim characteristics. In addition, there is a need for the incorporation 
of such surveys into victim treatment programs to test the efficacy 
of such programs. Third, there is the politicas-social control function: 
Decisions about the development of manpower and law enforcement 
are better served by victim survey data than incomplete and biased 
statistics of recorded crime.
As with any research strategy, there are methodological problems 
associated with victim surveys. The accuracy of victim recall of events 
is limited by memory failure, time telescoping, witholding or inventing 
information. Remedies for such sources of error include tests using 
different lengths of time periods and emphasis on structuring the time 
period for the respondents. Reverse record checks (i.e., selecting 
respondents from police reports) estimate the dimension of the telescop­
ing problem.
The limitation of victimization surveys is mainly the cost. Howeves 
it may be possible to reduce the cost of surveys by using a combination 
of techniques. For example, telephone or postal interviews can be used 
to screen out a victim population; personal interview can then be 
employed to obtain detailed information. Alternatively, questions can 
be added to an omnibus survey. This is called “piggy backing.”
Some general recommendations can be made on the future use 
of victim surveys:
1. 
Because victim surveys are relatively new, longitudinal studies 
should be developed to provide information over time for social policy­
makers. This will show whether victim reporting behavior and police 
recording behavior are constant. Thus, we can identify the extent to 
which changes in crime rates are the result of changes in reporting 
behavior and the extent to which reporting behavior interacts with 
police recording practices. This is a desirable but costly procedure.
2. 
Efforts should also be made to standardize questions about 
victimization in behavioral terms. This will facilitate cross-cultural 
comparisons of criminal behavior and its impact on victims.
3. 
In order to improve reliability and perhaps relate crime to 
other social factors, methodologists need to explore how different po­
pulations define different criminal behaviors. For example, a “funnelling” 
technique could be used in which interviews are initially asked 
about injures and only later about whether any injuries reported were 
the result of crime. In addition, methodological research should be 

---

APPENDICE VI
169
undertaken to provide a cost-benefit analysis of various experimental 
techniques.
4. 
Police methods of recording statistics could be improved to 
include both victim- and offender-related data to overcome some of the 
problems of comparing victim survey data with police statistics.
5. 
More effort should be made to tie victim surveys to geographical 
areas smaller than cities. Administrative units like census tracts are 
an example. A different methodological approach presented in Table 1 
is the use of archival or secondary data.
Victimization surveys were developed to avoid the problems of 
nonrandom loss from the occurrence of a crime through to its recording 
in police statistics. However, surveys are expensive and restricted in 
purpose. Accordingly, some of the more reliable archival sources should 
continue to be used. This is particularly necessary because neither 
survey nor archival data on crime or victim-related events are, or ever 
will be entirely valid or reliable.
Illustrations of the more reliable kinds of archival data are poliice 
reports of homicide and auto theft, and coroners’ reports of homicide 
and suicide victims. Longitudinal city case history and cross-sectional 
analysis can be carried out with such data. Thre analyses con relate 
victimization to macrostructural factors such as unemployment, inflation, 
ethnic and population distribution. Such historical studies also provide 
opportunities for much needed replication of findings.
The ready availability of archival data from police, and to a lesser 
extent from the courts, provides important material for evaluation re­
search. To develop more effective programs in the future, measures 
can be taken of policy and program impacts both retrospectively and 
for ongoing programs. For instance, measures can be obtained from 
coroners’ cause-of-death-files on the extent of death by drug overdose, 
cirrhosis of the liver for alcoholism, homicide, suicide, and accidents.
Archival records of any program or institution which deals with 
victims, such as the police, courts, or rape crisis centers, can be used 
to study the treatment of victims once they enter the program. Providing 
that information on the characteristics of the victims is included in these 
records, the differential handling of individuals by the program can 
be analyzed.
Substantive findings and implications
Previous discussion has focused on two specific methodologies. As 
an illustration of these and other methodological approaches four 

---

170
LA VITTIMA
substantive areas where some of these methods have been applied in 
the past will be considered.
The Role of the Victim. — One central question in the study 
of deviant behavior has been the role of the victim. To what extent 
does the victim help bring about, encourage, contribute to, or participate 
in his or her own victimization?
Methodological difficulties and previous findings: Relatively few 
attempts haw been made to define victim participation, operationalize 
the definition and empirically determine the frequency of participation 
for a sample of crime. With few exceptions, the empirical work that 
has been done is based on official records-usually police data. Most 
of the work is on violent crime or burglary in American cities. There 
is some comparative information — mainly on homicide and robbery — 
from countries in Africa, Canada, and India, Poland and the United 
Kingdom. The definition of victim participation in these studies usually 
varies from crime to crime, as well as from country to country. This 
available empirical information suggests that, for the time and place 
and for the definitions used, the victim plays a considerable role in 
homicide, assault, and probably also robbery. Contrary to many popular 
misconceptions, the data suggests that the victim’s role typically may 
be minimal in forcible rape.
Methodological needs in future work: The role of the victim can 
be approached better through concepts such as “victim participation” 
and “victim vulnerability” rather than by concepts such as “victim 
precipitation” and “victim provocation.”. Although often used in past 
research, the latter terms can be biased against the victim and, in the 
case of sexual crime, can be considered sexist. The former terms are 
more neutral, have potential for better approximating actual victim 
behavior, and tend to avoid stereotyped legal usages. Among other 
applications the notion of victim participation addresses acts of phy­
sical assault in which mutual conflict obscures and interchanges the 
roles of ultimate offender and ultimate victim. The idea, of victim 
vulnerability in sensitive to situations, short of open encouragement, 
in which the victim may not have taken adequate precautions, as is 
the case in sexual crime and theft.
Behavioral definitions of victim participation standardized across 
cultures should be developed for all crimes with victims. They should 
not be based on legal conceptions or on assumptions that certain 
structural contexts (like hitchiking for rape) are precipitative, per se. 
Degree of participation should be determined for a crime and the 
perception of independent observers, victims and offenders should be 
trin,.oulated (i.e., used jointly). However, it may be that only blatant 

---

appendice vi
171
physical actions, like striking the first blow in homicide, can be ope­
rationalized, whereas notions such as “suggestive gestures” in rape and 
“temptation opportunities” in robbery are too subtle or vague to be 
measured and replicated. In determining victim participation, police 
reports and other institutional filters should be avoided — or at least 
used as only one source of knowledge. Following these guidelines, 
a data base for degree of participation should be built up across different 
kinds of crimes in different countries. A continuous time series of 
information should be developed.
Implications : Once more substantive quantitative findings on victim 
participation become available, the victims role, if any, in a particular 
crime must be more completely integrated than at present with a theory 
of offender behavior. Quite the opposite to bringing on a crime, the 
action of the victim prior to the act or even after the offender initiates 
the act can prevent the attempt from being successfully completed. 
Accordingly, a theory of victim response to crime should be developed 
that is the conceptual mirror image of a theory of victim participation.
Policy directed at reducing participation by the victim may be more 
successful for crimes in which the probability of rational offender 
behavior is relatively higher (like burglary or robbery) than for crimes 
in wich the probability is relatively lower (such as homicide and 
assault). More refined information on victim participation is required 
to guide victim compensation policy.
2. 
The Reporting of Crimes. — Another area of substantial in­
terest to research is why persons contact the police to seek assistance 
for themselves or for others. There is a growing interest in the extent 
to which the police, the more general criminal justice aggregate, and 
other agencies (such as hospitals) are responding to the need of victims. 
Systematic research on these questions has been undertaken through 
surveys of crime victims and the general public, as well as in both 
laboratory and fields experiments.
Factors leading to an increase in reporting: Victimization surveys 
have indicated that some of the reasons people give for reporting crimes 
to the police are a moral duty and a desire to collect insurance. The 
surveys also reveal that severity of the crime is positively correlated with 
reporting. Experimental research on bystanders provides evidence that 
both the advice of others and prior commitment to report have a 
positive impact on reporting.
Factors leading to a decrease in reporting: Interviews with victims 
indicate that the reasons given for not reporting are related to belief 
in the ineffectiveness of the police, concern that it. was not a police 
matter and the difficulty in reporting. Experimental evidence again 

---

172
LA VITTIMA
indicates that others are effective in persuading bystanders not to report 
crimes.
Factors apparently unrelated to crime reporting: A number of 
studies have indicated that variables thought to have an impact on 
reporting actually appear to be unrelated to reporting. Survey and 
experimental research have found no relationship between reporting 
and attitudes toward the police, or attitudes toward the offender. These 
methods have also failed to uncover any systematic relationship between 
victim characteristics and reporting. Surveys generally show that fear of 
reprisal is very infrequently cited as a reason for not reporting. Some 
experimental research has filed to find any effect of mass media campaigns 
to affect reporting.
Future research: There are many knowledge gaps. For instance, 
it is still not known whether community cohesiveness, special rewards 
for bystanders, or assurance of anonymity can influence reporting. Pro­
vision of restitution, compensation, insurance or special facilities and 
arrangements for victims and witnesses has an unknown impact on 
reporting. Surprisingly, such matters as past experience with the criminal 
system, fear of crime, prior criminality of the victim, and relationship 
between the victim and offender have all been examined without any 
generalizable conclusions. Nor is it known how victims differ from 
bystanders in reporting. Nor has research been done on the speed with 
which persons report (a factor apparently related to police effectiveness).
Implications: Based upon past research findings, it would appear 
that programs which encourage citizens to feel a moral commitment to 
report crimes would be effective especially if introduced early in the 
socialization process. Programs which provide for a public commitment 
would also appear to be worthwhile. However, programs which depended 
solely on mass media would appear not to have much impact on 
crime reporting.
Because many victims state that they did not report a crime because 
they did not consider it a police matter, a reconsideration of the 
definition of particular crimes by legislators may be called for. Police 
and victims may not have to feel that all crimes (particularly less 
serious) must be uncovered and prosecuted. However, when the public 
reports to the police, special efforts should be made to not only take 
a report but to provide feedback on progress made.
3. 
The Victim and the Criminal Justice System. — As we have 
seen, the victim’s first impact on the criminal justice system is his/her 
decision to report the crime. If the victim decides to report, a relation­
ship develops between the victim and the system. This relationship can 
be conceptualized as at least three separate but interrelated research 

---

APPENDICE VI
173
areas. Although little empirical research has been completed in any 
of these areas, there are some questions that can be raised.
How do victims affect the criminal justice system through their 
decisions? Victims in the role of witnesses present one illustration of 
this question. According to analyses of prosecutor records, a significant 
cause of case attrition is the failure of witnesses to testify. Hence, the 
reasons explaining victim/witness cooperation must be explored more 
systematically, including the types of victims who are labeled as “non­
cooperators” and the reasons for their behavior. One method for studying 
such behavior would be a follow-back survey of victims who have had 
contract with the court process. Another illustration of the question 
deals with compensation and restitution programs. There is a need to 
know who is utilizing existing programs. A survey of victims could 
include a question about whether the victim is aware of the existence 
or has actually applied to such boards.
How does the criminal justice system take the victim into account 
when making decisions? This question addresses, for example, the effects 
of victim characteristics on decisions to charge, prosecute or convict. 
Research in the past has concentrated on the police decision to charge 
and the jury’s decision to convict. A recent analysis including prosecutors 
decisions as well as this past research has shown that characteristics of 
the victim have an impact on decision making. These studies have either 
been based on participant observations and are thus subject to the 
interpretations of observers, or they have used prosecutor or court 
records which are subject to the interpretation of the person filling out 
the form used for collecting data.
Experimental data from the field of social psychology has also made 
a contribution to knowledge in this area. Data primarily from college 
students simulating the behavior of judges or jury members indicate 
that the perception of the victim affects hypothetical actions to be taken 
against the defendent. Although the experimental design of such studies 
allows the researcher to control the situation, in order to analyze 
whether a particular variable makes a difference, there is a problem in 
generalizing these results to actual decision makers.
In the future, more use could be made of court records, preferably 
automated, to exaimne which characteristics of the victim make a differ­
ence. In such analyses, many control variables must be included. 
Another method is to ask prosecutors which characteristics they take 
into account. Both methods can provide insight particularly if done in 
the same jurisdiction.
How is the victim affected by the criminal justice process? In 
particular, this question addresses the efficacy of special victim assistance 

---

174
LA VITTIMA
programs. There have been only a few empirical studies of the effects 
of the criminal justice system on victims. There are also anecdotal 
impressions, particularly in rape cases, but as yet little systematic 
research effort. One relevant study is a follow-back survey of witnesses 
in which a large percentage indicated that they would not participate 
as witnesses again after a contact with the court process. Other studies 
are using survey and observational methods to determine both the 
needs and the complaints of victims and witnesses. There needs to 
be more research to identify problem areas particularly in crimes other 
than rape. Surveys of victims would probably be the best method of 
use, because the subjective experience of the victim needs to be measured. 
Victim/witness programs should be evaluated using an experimental 
design. Victims randomly assigned to such programs can be compared 
with a control group in order to assess any impacts.
Implications'. There are implications for both the victim and the 
criminal justice system in doing research on their interrelationship. The 
victims can receive treatment and consideration related to their needs as 
identified through research, particularly in terms of their experiences 
with police and courts as a witness. In addition, the criminal justice 
system can benefit from knowing how the victim can best be dealt with 
as a witness. Be evaluating special victim assistance programs which 
are developed through research, only those considered to be effective 
can be continued.
Overall Conclusions
1. 
Police and treatment decisions should be based, whenever 
possible on research findings. Thus, policy planning should include 
a time perspective needed to conduct the necessary research.
2. 
Programs designed to deal with victimization should include 
an evaluation component. The sophistication of the evaluation should 
be appropriate to the type and extent of the program.
3. Many of the methodologies raise ethical questions concerning 
deception, manipulation and invasion of privacy. Researchers should 
be sensitive to these issues. However, empirical research should be 
conducted to measure the extent and seriousness of the various problems 
discussed in this paper. Policy cannot depend solely on value judgments.
4. Whenever possible, victimological research should use more 
than one methodological approach.
5. 
Finally, the entire area of the relationship between the victim 
and the criminal justice system should be more fully explored through 
systematic quantitative research.

---

APPENDICE VI
175
III. Treatment and Prevention of Victimization
This section was developed by the members of the Treatment and 
Prevention Task Force, including Concetta Stallone Adleman (USA), 
Alvin Cohn (USA), John Dussich (USA), David Friedman (USA), 
Sherry Icenhower (USA), Mary Keefe (USA), Hidemichi Morosawa 
(Japan), and Robert Towell (USA).
While it was realized that there is merit in examining victimization 
from different perspectives — criminological, psychological, ecological 
and industrial — the state of knowledge was reviewed primarily from 
the criminological perspective and, only secondarily, from a noncrimi- 
nological viewpoint.
Because of the expertise of certain members of the group, an agree­
ment was reached to explore the significance of victimization with regard 
to rape, child abuse and murder. Such issues as the victimization of 
offenders by the criminal justice system and the victimization of elderly 
persons were also touched upon.
While the perspective was primarily Western, the contributions 
of the Japanese representative did provide a certain level of interna­
tionalism to this report.
The development of a victimological approach to treatment and 
prevention issues demands that some attention be paid to definitions. 
Thus, the treatment and prevention task force developed a working 
definition of victimization to mean the exploitation and/or abuse of one 
or more persons by others.
While it is recognized that all victimization does not involve an 
exploitative relationship between victim and victimizer, there at least 
exists, in most cases, some level of abuse between parties.
The definition of prevention upon which the following material is 
based is that of the attempt to prevent or otherwise keep victimization 
from occuring. Treatment, then, becomes a broad concept by which 
some attempt is made, once victimization has occurred, to alter, cure 
or alleviate those conditions which result as a consequence. Insofar as 
treatment is concerned, it is also recognized that there are at least three 
levels of application: for the victim; for those persons associated with 
the victim or affected by the victimization; and for the offender. In 
the latter instance, it is also recognized that when treatment efforts are 
made on behalf of the offender, this effort in and of itself may be 
interpreted as prevention.
A model was developed to examine treatment and prevention issues 
of victimization, with the except that, once created it can be applied 
to other victimological situations as well. The purpose of the heuristic 
model is to provide a viable method for addressing primary aspects of 

---

176
LA VITTIMA
treatment and prevention. It should be viewed as a process that attempts 
to integrate comprehensively the numerous program components of 
treatment or prevention as well as the parameters for addressing these 
two activities.
The model uses the following format: the state of the art (know­
ledge about the subject); problems; and policy and recommendations. 
These aspects were looked at in terms of function, defined as goals and 
objects; structures, defined as organizations delivering services; and 
resources, such as manpower and funds, necessary to deliver services.
The model is presented here using rape prevention illustratively 
(see Table 2). The illustration is not to be interpreted as being exhaustive; 
it is presented merely as a working example of how the paradigm can 
be used. It is incumbent upon the reader to remember that all programs, 
policies, and specific recommendations must reflect the human needs and 
concerns of the total person, rather than agency needs and concerns. 
This would mean, especially in the case of rape, that any preventive or 
treatment interventions must respect the victim and those other persons 
affected by the victimization. Additionally, it is to be pointed out that 
programmatic efforts must also avoid the development of secondary 
victimization. That is, in the case of the rapist, attention must be paid 
to the avoidance of turning the victimizer into the victimized.
A MODEL TO EXAMINE THE PREVENTION OF CRIME

In reviewing the model to examine victimization developed by the 
Treatment and Prevention Task Force, Mary L. Keefe and Concetta 
S. Adleman decided to test its efficacy in terms of their knowledge 
and expertise on the subject of rape as the Commanding Officer of the 
Sex Crimes Analysis Unit and a Psychiatric Nurse-Crisis Intervention 
Trainer in New York City. While the group report demonstrates the 
model in a broad context, the applied it to case studies by drawing 
upon their experience in treating rape victims (see Tables 3 and 4).
Skilled crisis intervention is viewed here as essential to the rape 
victim’s recovery. Police officers and psychiatric nurses can draw upon 
their experiences and can also be further trained to effectively handle 
victims in crisis.
The many implications for the use of the model in the prevention 
and treatment of victimization are outline in Table 3.

IV. The Victim and the Justice System
This section was prepared by the following Institute members: 
Frederick Dubow (USA), Ezzet A. Fattah (Canada), Barbara Hafer 
(USA), Nancy Holman (USA) Akira Ishii (Japan), Le Roy L. Lambom 
(USA), Anna Laszlo (USA), Blanca Nieve Marin (Argentina), Hazel 
L. Meenhorst-Blok (Netherlands), Nwokocka K. U. Nkpa (Nigeria), 
and Antony Schembri (USA).
At present many victims of crime do not receive fair treatment 
from the criminal justice system. In many cases they are ignored, 
delayed, financially burdened, traumatized, or stigmatized by that system. 
It is understandable, therefore, that many crime victims are reluctant 
to report the offense to the authorities testify against the accused, or 
cooperate otherwise with the criminal justice system. Changes of attitude 
and practices by those working in the system are necessary if victim 
cooperation with and attitudes toward the system are to be improved.
Victims of crime are entitled to a fair responsive, and sympathetic 
hearing. Victim interactions with the criminal justice system should be 
made less costly, traumatizing, and stigmatizing for them. Facilities 
presently available to offenders, such as legal aid and medical and 
psychiatric services, should be extended to victims of crime. Moreover, 
innovative and imaginative services should be developed to meet the 
specific needs of victims. Victim participation in proceedings should be 
facilitated by provision of transportation, child care, and payment for 
expenses, and by reduction of other hardships that interfere with victim 
participation.
Better training is necessary for those working in the criminal justice 
system; greater emphasis should be placed on understanding and dealing 
with victims. Interviewing the child victim of crime poses special 
problems. It must be performed with great care by adequately trained 
professional interviewers.

---

APPENDICE VI
183
The operations and impact of victim-witness assistance programs 
and other programs designed to increase victim and witness cooperation 
with the criminal justice system should be evaluated. The advisability 
of new institutional roles for the victim, such as legal standing in the 
trial of the accused and other participation in the system consonant with 
the rights of the defendant should be explored. Te potential impact 
of increased community participation and use of lay workers in the 
criminal justice system on the community’s attitude toward victims and 
on victims’ attitudes toward the criminal justice system should be 
investigated.
Growing concern for the victim, in particular the victim of sex 
offenses, should not compromise the safeguards provided by law for 
the accused. A balance among the needs and rights of the victim, the 
offender, and society must be established and maintained.
The offender’s awareness of the victim is in many cases diminished 
because of rejection of the victim. A change in the offender’s attitude 
toward the victim may be necessary for the success of the correctional 
process. By placing greater emphasis on restitution and compensation 
society is likely to enhance the offender’s awareness of the victim, and 
may increase the success of the correctional process. Bringing together 
the offender and the victim in the judicial or correctional process may 
have therapeutic value in certain cases and is apt to sensitize the of­
fender to the plight of his victim. Informal means of reconciliation and 
arbitration, in which victim and offender interact directly in the presence 
of a third party, should be encouraged in many cases. Such efforts may 
lead to considerable savings of cost and manpower and alleviate the 
case load of an already overburdened criminal justice system. They may 
also provide solutions that are better suited to victim needs and may 
restore harmony to family relationships distrupted by an offense. Intra­
family victimization and the appropriateness of judicial solutions to 
that problem should be reexamined; more effective alternatives to the 
present system should be found.
Compensation
Because victims of crimes seldom receive adequate financial re­
paration from other sources, state comprensation is necessary to alleviate 
their plight and to achieve social justice.
State compensation is a meaningful and visible demonstration of 
societal concern that criminal wrongs be righted. Public insurance 
schemes do not serve this particular objective. To compensate victims 
of crime and victims of automobile accidents from the same insurance 

---

184
la vittima
scheme may blur the distinction between crime and negligence and 
accident.
Although full compensation for criminal injury or loss is to be 
regarded as the ultimate goal of any state compensation system, limi­
tations on financial resources may require the establishment of priorities. 
Primary concern should be for victims of intentional crimes of violence 
resulting in the death of or injury to victims. Adequate compensations 
would repair direct damages incurred by victims, including the cost 
of necessary treatment and hospitalization and loss of eamings; it would 
provide support for dependents of deceased victims, as well as reasonable 
financial compensation for pain and suffering.
Establishment of a maximum or a minimum level of compensation 
poses an arbitrary restriction on the principle of adequate compensation. 
Direct damages should be fully compensated, although limitations on 
financial resources may require establishment of reasonable maxima on 
compensation for loss of eamings, support of dependents of deceased 
victims, and pain and suffering.
Although reimbursement for direct damages already incurred and 
any payment for pain and suffering should be in lump sum, compensation 
for loss of eamings during the period of disability and for support of 
dependents of deceased victims during the period of dependency should 
be by regular periodic payments to be reviewed periodically for possible 
adjustments.
Compensation should be awarded to victims for direct damages, 
including loss of eamings, and to dependents of deceased victims for 
loss of support regardless of the victim’s contribution to the genesis 
of the crime. However, victim precipitation of or participation in the 
crime may be a reason for denying or reducing the amount of com­
pensation for pain and suffering.
The relationship between offender and victim should not be a basis 
for rendering the victim ineligible for compensation. However, com­
pensation should be conditioned on the victim’s cooperation with the 
criminal justice system. The burden of proof should be on the victim 
and other safeguards should reduce the possibility of fraud and collusion.
Because one goal of victim compensation is demonstration of 
societal concern that criminal wrongs be righted, the financial situation 
of the victim should be immaterial to the decision whether compensation 
is to be awarded or denied. The victim’s financial situation may, 
however, properly be considered regarding the amount of compensation.
Insanity, infancy, intoxication, and other disabilities of the offender, 
as well as his acquittal, should not be reasons for denying compensation 
to victims who otherwise would be eligible for it.

---

APPENDICE VI
185
The availability of other sources of reparation for the victim should 
not be a reason for denying him compensation. However, amounts the 
victim receives from the offender or from government sources should 
be deducted from state compensation to the extent that the victim 
would otherwise profit from the offense.
Reimbursement by the offender to the government of the amount 
paid to the victim is desirable as long as such payments do not interfere 
with the correctional process.
Wide publicity should be given to victim compensation programs 
so that victims are aware of their existence. The establishment of the 
compensation bodies should ensure easy geographical access by all 
victims.
Procedures for state compensation should not be so formal as 
to deter applications but should be formal enough to ensure a fair 
hearing for both victim and offender and an equitable decision, and to 
facilitate review by an appellate structure. Procedures for state com­
pensation should neither be cumbersome nor impose indignities upon 
applicants. A form of legal aid should be available so that needy victims 
may obtain legal assistance without additional financial burden. Pro­
cedures for state compensation should be expedient without compromis­
ing the right of both victim and offender to a fair hearing. Emergency 
payments should be available for the immediate needs of the victim, 
irrespective of the final decision on the application.
State compensation should be awarded by an autonomous administra­
tive agency rather than the judge handling the criminal trial. Amounts 
received from fines, forfeitures, and subrogation should be reserved 
in a special fund for the purpose of providing adequate sources for 
state compensation.
Compensation boards should be required to keep adequate records 
and to publish detailed periodic reports of their activities so that 
evaluation of the operations and efficacy of compensation schemes will 
be facilitated. The effectiveness of the different compensation systems and 
their ramifications for the criminal justice system should be compared.
Restitution
Restitution — payment by the offender for or his working to 
repair the dagame done to the victim — recognizes the victim’s needs 
and reaffirms social values.
Restitution may be used as a sanction in its own right. In many 
cases restitution may be main sanction imposed by the court. It may 
be an alternative to imprisonment or used in addition to other sanctions. 
Restitution should be given full consideration as an alterative to adju­

---

186
LA VITTIMA
dication as well as in pre-trial settlement procedures. It may be a 
condition of probation and may also be a factor in determining release 
from imprisonment. To the extent feasible the victim should be given 
the opportunity to participate in all proceedings in which restitution 
is decided.
The relationship between the victim and offender should not auto­
matically bar an order for restitution by the offender.
The offender’s ability to make restitution should be given attention 
at several different stages. If the combination of court-ordered restitution 
and a fine is likely to place a great financial burden on the offender, 
the court should give priority to restitution. If the offender does not 
have the means to comply with a restitution order, he should be given 
an opportunity to work for the victim or some other person or agency. 
The work could be service in lieu of payment or the offender could be 
paid the going wage and be required to make restitution payments from 
his wages. Offenders sentenced to imprisonment should have the oppor­
tunity to work in the penal institution at reasonable wages, a portion 
of which should be available for restitution payments. Work-release 
programs are meaningful ways of providing offenders the ability to 
make restitution payments. Arbitrary or undue restrictions should not 
be placed upon employment of former inmates, so that restitution pay­
ments will not be discontinued.
The extent, patterning, and effectiveness of the various judicial 
and nonjudicial restitution practices and programs should be investigated. 
The effect of victim compensation and victim restitution schemes on 
victim’s reporting of crimes and on victim cooperation with the criminal 
justice system should be evaluated.
